Executor's Transaction Limits and Ghabn Fahish
Ruling on executor's sale/purchase limits with ghabn definition, plus comparison with authorized persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissible price range for an executor's transactions, define ghabn fahish and ghabn yasir, and distinguish between an executor and an authorized person regarding excessive ghabn.

Core Idea
An executor must avoid excessive fraud; authorized persons may engage in it because they act by their own capacity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the limits of an executor's authority when trading on behalf of a minor. The core principle is that the executor must act in the minor's best interest, which prohibits transacting with excessive price differences. The text also compares the executor's position with that of an authorized minor or slave, who have greater freedom. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of guardianship in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the initial sentence: 'An executor shall neither sell nor buy except with what people overlook.' This establishes the general restriction: the executor's transactions must be within the range of slight price differences.
This is a general rule; exceptions follow.
2
Understand the definition of ghabn
The text defines ghabn yasir (slight fraud) as what falls under the valuation of expert valuators, and ghabn fahish (excessive fraud) as what does not. Learn this distinction.
This is a practical, expert-based criterion.
3
Compare executor with authorized persons
The text contrasts the executor with an authorized minor/slave/mukatib. Identify the key difference: the executor acts as a deputy (niyaba), while authorized persons act by their own legal capacity after removal of interdiction.
This explains why authorized persons are not bound by the same restriction according to Abu Hanifa.
4
Analyze the self-buying exception
The passage allows a father-appointed executor to buy from himself if there is a manifest benefit for the minor. Note the two conditions: must be father-appointed (not qadi-appointed) and benefit must be clear.
This is a narrow exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يبيع وصي ولا يشتري إلا بما يُتَغَابَنُ (به) ، اعلم أنه يجوز للوصي أن يـ مال الصبي، وهو من المنقولات من الأجنبي بمثل القيمةِ وبما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه وهو ما يَدْخُلُ تحت تقويم المقومين، ويجوز أن يشتري له من الأجنبي كذلك ، لا بالغبن الفاحش ) ، وأما الاشتراء من نفسه، فإن كان الوصي وصي الأب يجوز ؛ لا إن كان وصي القاضي، لكن بشرط أن يكون للصغير فيه منفعة ظاهرة". [ 1 ] قوله : وبما يتغابن الناس فيه ؛ غبنا فاحشاً ، اعلم أنّ الفقهاء اختلفوا في تعريف الغبن الفاحش والغبن اليسير، والأصح ما قيل في الفرق بينهما أن ما يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين فهو غبن يسير، وما لا يدخلُ فهو غبن فاحش. كما يفهم من تقرير «النهاية». [٢] قوله : لا بالغبن الفاحش ؛ لأنه لا نظر في الغبن الفاحش، بخلاف اليسير؛ لأنه لا يمكن التحرز عنه، ففي اعتبار التحرز عن اليسير انسداد باب الوصاية والصبي المأذونُ والعبد المأذونُ والمكاتب يجوز بيعهم وشراؤهم بالغبن الفاحش عند الأعظم ؛ لأنهم يتصرفون بأهليتهم لا بأمر المولى أو الأب ؛ لأن الإذن فك الحجر، فلم يكن تصرفهم نيابة عن أحد. بخلاف الوصي ؛ لأنه يتصرف بحكم النيابة الشرعية نظراً، ولا نظر في إتلاف المال، فيتقيد بموضع النظر ، وعندهما لا يملكونه ؛ لأنَّ التصرُّفَ بالفاحش منه : نه تبرع لا ضرورة فيه، وهم ليسوا من أهل التبرع. كذا في «الهداية»(۱). [٣] قوله : منفعة ظاهرة ؛ قال الزيلعي : تفسير المنفعة الظاهرة أن يبيع ما خمسة عشر بعشرة من الصغير، أو يشتري ما يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر لنفسه من مال الصغير ، وهو مختار الشارح له في «المنتقى»، وبه يفتي. وفي «الخانية»: ولهذا فسَّرَ الخيرية الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له في غير العقار، وهي في العقار عند البعض أن يشتري بضعف القيمة، ويبيع بنصفها ، وفي «الحافظية»: يجوز بيع
(An executor shall neither sell nor buy except with what people overlook (i.e., a slight difference). Know that it is permissible for the executor to manage the property of the minor, and it is from movables from a stranger at the equivalent of the value and with what people overlook, which is what falls under the valuation of valuators, and it is permissible to buy for him from a stranger likewise, not with excessive fraud (ghabn fahish). As for buying from himself: if the executor is the father's executor, it is permissible; not if he is the qadi's executor, but with the condition that there be a manifest benefit for the minor. [1] His statement: 'with what people overlook' (i.e., a fraud that is excessive). Know that the jurists differed in defining excessive fraud and slight fraud. The most correct is what is said in the distinction between them: what falls under the valuation of valuators is slight fraud, and what does not fall is excessive fraud, as understood from the exposition of al-Nihayah. [2] His statement: 'not with excessive fraud' because there is no consideration (nazar) in excessive fraud, contrary to slight fraud because one cannot guard against it; for considering guarding against slight fraud would close the door of executorship. The authorized minor, the authorized slave, and the mukatib are permitted to sell and buy with excessive fraud according to the Greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) because they act by their own legal capacity (ahliyyah), not by the order of the master or father; because permission is removal of interdiction, so their action is not deputyship for anyone. Contrary to the executor, because he acts by virtue of legal deputyship as a matter of consideration (nazar), and there is no consideration in wasting wealth, so he is restricted to the place of consideration. According to the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they (the authorized minor, slave, and mukatib) do not have that power because acting with excessive fraud is a donation without necessity, and they are not qualified for donation. As in al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'manifest benefit'; al-Zayla'i said: the interpretation of manifest benefit is that he sells what is worth fifteen for ten from the minor, or buys what is worth ten for fifteen for himself from the minor's wealth, and it is the choice of the commentator in al-Muntaqa, and according to it fatwa is given. In al-Khaniyyah: for this reason Imam al-Sarakhsi interpreted it as a good deal in non-real estate, and in real estate according to some he buys at double the value and sells at half. In al-Hafiziyyah: it is permissible to sell...
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that an executor (wasi) must transact for the minor at a price that is within the range of slight difference (ghabn yasir), i.e., what people normally overlook in mutual dealings. The scholars define 'excessive fraud' (ghabn fahish) as any price difference that does not fall within the range of valuation by experts (muqawwimin). Slight fraud is within that range. The reason: an executor acts as a legal deputy (niyaba shar'iyya) with the duty to act in the minor's best interest (nazar). Excessive fraud wastes wealth, which is contrary to that duty. In contrast, a minor who has been authorized to trade (al-sabi al-ma'dhun), an authorized slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun), and a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) are permitted to trade with excessive fraud according to Abu Hanifa because they act by their own legal capacity (ahliyyah) after removal of interdiction (hajr); their action is not deputyship. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold these individuals cannot trade with excessive fraud because it is essentially a donation (tabarru'), and they are not qualified for donation. The passage also addresses a special case: an executor appointed by the father may buy from himself if there is a manifest benefit (manfa'ah zahirah), e.g., selling an item worth fifteen for ten from the minor, or buying an item worth ten for fifteen. For the qadi-appointed executor, this is not allowed. The manifest benefit is defined by al-Zayla'i and adopted by the commentator of al-Muntaqa. Al-Sarakhsi limits this to movables; for real estate, some say he may buy at double and sell at half.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Excessive fraud where the price difference does not fall within expert valuation.
وصي الأب (wasi al-ab): An executor appointed by the father; has broader powers including buying from himself with benefit.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A minor or slave who is permitted by guardian/master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes slight fraud (ghabn yasir) from excessive fraud (ghabn fahish)?
A) A fixed percentage of 10%
B) Whether it falls under expert valuation
C) Whether the transaction is with a stranger
D) The intent of the executor

Key Takeaway
An executor must transact within the range of slight price differences as determined by experts; authorized persons acting on their own capacity are not bound by this restriction according to Abu Hanifa.

Guardian's Basic Powers: Hawala, Loan, and Sale of Movables
Explains guardian's permissible actions and the istihsan basis for selling movable property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a guardian accepting hawala, lending, and selling movable property to an absent major, and explain the istihsan reasoning that permits the sale.

Core Idea
Guardians preserve, not profit: accept hawala from solvent, never lend, sell movables by istihsan.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes the foundational limits of a guardian's authority. The guiding principle is preservation (ḥifẓ). We examine three key actions: accepting a debt transfer, lending, and selling movable property. The commentary reveals that while qiyas would forbid the sale, istihsan permits it because movables are perishable and sale serves preservation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions
Locate the three actions discussed: hawala (accept transfer of debt), qard (lending to others), and bay' (selling) to a major absent person.
The text explicitly states rulings for each.
2
Extract the ruling for each
For hawala: permissible only from solvent debtor (al-amla'), not insolvent. For lending: absolutely prohibited. For sale: permissible for movables but not real estate.
The exception for real estate is a key distinction.
3
Understand the rationale for each ruling
Hawala from solvent facilitates collection without risk. Lending is not preservation and harms the orphan. Sale of movables is allowed because movables perish quickly; preserving the price is easier.
The principle of preservation (ḥifẓ) unifies all rulings.
4
Analyze the dalil: istihsan over qiyas
The text states that qiyas would prohibit sale of movable property, just as the father cannot sell over a major. But istihsan permits it because preservation requires it, and the guardian is entrusted with preservation.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتال على الأملأ لا على الأعسر، ولا يقرض ويبيع على الكبير الغائب إلا العقار وجاز للأب لا لوكيله ولا للوصي بيعُ مالِ أحدِ الصغيرين من الآخر، ولو وكل الأب وكيلين بذلك جاز، وفي بيع القاضي ذلك خلاف. ولو وكل الأب رجلاً يبيع ماله من طفلِهِ أو الشراء منه لم يجز إلا إذا كان الأب حاضراً، ولم يجز للقاضي بيعُ مال اليتيم من نفسه، وعكسه إذ الجواز من القاضي على وجه الحكم، ولا يجوز حكمه لنفسه، بخلاف ما شراهُ من وصيه أو باعه من اليتيم ، وقبل وصيه فإنه يجوز، ولو وصيّاً من جهة هذا القاضي... الخ، ملخصاً. كذا في «رد المحتار). [١]قوله : ويحتال ؛ أي يقبلُ الحوالة في قبض الدين الذي كان لليتيم على الآخر. [٢] قوله : على الأملأ ؛ أي على الأقدر على أداء الدين من المحيل وهو المديون. [٣]قوله : ولا يقرض ؛ أي لا يقرضُ الوصي مال اليتيم ؛ لأنَّ المفوض إليه الحفظ والقرض ليس من الحفظ، بل فيه ضرر اليتيم فلا يجوز. [٤]قوله : إلا العقار ؛ أي يجوز للوصي أن يبيع على الكبير الغائب في كلّ شيء إلا العقار، قيد الكبير بالغيبة ؛ لأنهم إذا كانوا حضوراً ليس للوصي التصرف في التركةِ أصلاً، لكن يتقاضى ديون الميت، ويقبضُ حقوقه، ويدفع إلى الورثة، إلا إذا كان على الميت دين أو أوصى بوصيّة ولم يقض الورثة الديون، ولم ينفذوا الوصية من مالهم، فإنه يبيع التركة. كذا في «العناية». ثم استثنى المصنف العقار في حق الكبير الغائب، أما في حق الصغير فيملك الوصي بيع العقار أيضاً، وهذا جواب السلف. وأما جواب المتأخرين أنه إنما يجوز بأحدِ شروط ثلاثة : إما أن يرغب المشتري فيه بضعف القيمة، أو للصغير حاجة إلى أثمنها، أو ما يكون على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا به ، قال الصدر الشهيد الله : وبه يفتي. كذا في «الكفاية». (1) قوله : لأن بيع ماله.... الخ ؛ وكان القياس أن لا يملك الوصي بيع غيره غير العقار أيضاً ؛ لأنه لا يملكه الأبُ على الكبير، إلا أنا استحسناه لما أنه حفظ ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه، وحفظ الثمن أيسر، وهو يملك الحفظ. أما العقار فمحصن بنفسه، فلا حاجة فيه إلى البيع ، ولو كان عليه دين يبيعُ العقار، ثم إن كان الدين مستغرقاً باعَ كله بالإجماع، وإن لم يكن مستغرقاً باع بقدر الدين عندهما ؛ لعدم الحاجة إلى أكثر من ذلك. وعند الأعظم الله جاز له بيعُ كلّه ؛ لأنه يبيعه بحكم الولاية، فإذا ثبت في البعض ثبت في الكلّ ؛ لأنها لا تتجزأ، ولو خيف هلاكه يملك بيعه ؛ لأنه تعين حفظه كالمنقول والأصح أنه لا يملك ؛ لأنه نادر. كذا أفاد الزيلعي ) [٢]قوله : ولا يتجر في ماله ؛ لأنّ المفوض إليه... الخ ؛ قال قاضي خان : ولا يجوز للوصي أن يتجر لنفسه بمال اليتيم والميت، فإن فعل وربح ضَمِنَ رأس المال ، ويتصدق بالربح في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له سلّم الربح، ولا يتصدق بشيء. انتهى. [٣] قوله : ووصي أب الطفل أحقُّ بماله من الجد ؛ هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي له الجد أحق ؛ لأنَّ الشرع أقامه مقامَ الأب عند عدمه، حتى أحرز الميراث، فيقدم على وصي الأب.
He may accept a transfer of debt (hawala) from the solvent one, not from the insolvent; he may not lend, and he may sell to a major absent person except real estate. It is permissible for the father, but not for his agent nor the guardian, to sell the property of one minor to another; if the father appoints two agents for that, it is permissible; regarding the judge's sale of that, there is a difference. If the father appoints a man to sell his property to his child or to buy from him, it is not permissible unless the father is present; it is not permissible for the judge to sell the orphan's property to himself; the opposite [is the case] because permissibility from the judge is by way of judgment, and his judgment for himself is not permissible, unlike what he buys from his guardian or sells to the orphan, or accepts the guardian's [sale], for it is permissible; even if [the judge is] a guardian appointed by this judge... etc., summarized. So in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: 'wa yah'tāl' – meaning he accepts the hawala in collecting the debt that was owed to the orphan from the other. [2] His saying: 'ala al-amla'' – meaning the one most capable of paying the debt from the transferor (muhīl), who is the debtor. [3] His saying: 'wa la yuqrid' – meaning the guardian does not lend the orphan's wealth because he is entrusted with preservation, and lending is not preservation; rather it involves harm to the orphan, so it is not permissible. [4] His saying: 'illa al-'iqār' – meaning it is permissible for the guardian to sell to a major absent person in everything except real estate; [the author] restricted [the ruling] to the major by absence because if they are present, the guardian has no authority to dispose of the estate at all; but he collects the debts of the deceased, receives his rights, and pays to the heirs, unless the deceased had a debt or bequeathed a will and the heirs did not pay the debts or execute the will from their own wealth; then he sells the estate. So in al-'Ināyah. Then the author excepted real estate in the case of a major absent person; as for the case of a minor, the guardian owns the sale of real estate as well – this is the answer of the predecessors. As for the answer of the later scholars, it is that it is permissible only under one of three conditions: either the buyer desires it at less than its value, or the minor has a need for its price, or the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid except through it. Al-Sadr al-Shahīd said: and by this fatwa is given. So in al-Kifāyah. (1) His saying: 'because selling his property...' etc. The qiyās would be that the guardian does not own the sale of other than real estate as well, because the father does not own it over the major; but we applied istihsān because it is preservation, since corruption quickly reaches it, and preserving the price is easier, and he owns preservation. As for real estate, it is fortified by itself, so there is no need to sell it. If there is a debt, he sells real estate. Then if the debt is comprehensive, he sells all of it by consensus; if not comprehensive, he sells only to the extent of the debt according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad) – because there is no need for more than that. According to the Greatest (Imām), he is permitted to sell all of it because he sells by virtue of guardianship, so if it is established for part, it is established for the whole, because it is indivisible. If its destruction is feared, he owns its sale because its preservation is determined like movable property; but the most correct is that he does not own it because it is rare. So related al-Zayla‘ī. [2] His saying: 'and he does not trade with his wealth' – because he is entrusted with preservation... etc. Qāḍī Khān said: It is not permissible for the guardian to trade for himself with the wealth of the orphan or the deceased; if he does so and makes a profit, he guarantees the capital, and he gives the profit in charity according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muhammad; according to Abū Yūsuf, he takes the profit and does not give anything in charity. End. [3] His saying: 'The guardian of the minor's father has more right to his wealth than the grandfather' – this is according to us; according to al-Shāfi‘ī, the grandfather has more right, because the Sharī‘a placed him in the position of the father when he is absent, even inheriting [through him], so he is preferred over the father's guardian.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the limits of a guardian's (wasī) authority over an orphan's property. The core principle is that the guardian is entrusted with preservation (ḥifẓ), not profit or speculation. Several rulings are derived from this principle. First, the guardian may accept a transfer of debt (ḥawāla) only from a solvent debtor (al-amla'), not an insolvent one, because this facilitates collection without risk. Second, he may not lend the orphan's money, as lending is not preservation and involves potential harm. Third, he may sell the orphan's movable property to a major absent person (al-kabīr al-ghā'ib) but not real estate (al-'iqār) according to the matn; the commentary explains that real estate is 'fortified by itself' (muḥṣan bi-nafsihi) and thus needs no sale for preservation. However, for a minor, earlier scholars (salaf) allowed sale of real estate, while later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) restricted it to three conditions: (1) the buyer offers less than market value, (2) the minor has a genuine need for the cash, or (3) the deceased's debts cannot be paid otherwise – and this is the fatwa position. The commentary also discusses the father's unique authority to buy/sell between his minor children, which his agent or guardian cannot do without his presence. Regarding trading (tijāra) with the orphan's wealth, it is prohibited for the guardian to trade for his own account; if he does and profits, he guarantees the principal and gives the profit in charity according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muhammad al-Shaybānī, while Abū Yūsuf allows him to keep the profit. Finally, a dispute with al-Shāfi‘ī is noted: in the Hanafi school, the father's appointed guardian takes precedence over the grandfather in managing the minor's wealth, whereas al-Shāfi‘ī gives priority to the grandfather because the Sharī‘a treats him as the father's substitute in inheritance.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-waṣī): The guardian appointed by the father (or judge) to manage the minor's property and affairs.
الأملأ (al-amla’): The person most capable of paying a debt; the solvent debtor.
الحوالة (al-ḥawāla): Transfer of debt from one person to another; the guardian may accept it only from a solvent debtor.
العقار (al-'iqār): Immovable property (real estate), which is 'fortified by itself' and thus generally not to be sold by the guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why may the guardian sell movable property of the orphan to an absent major?
A) Because the father also has that right.
B) Because of istihsan based on preservation, as movables are perishable.
C) Because the major absent person has consented.
D) Because the judge has given permission.

Key Takeaway
A guardian's authority over orphan's property is governed by preservation: hawala from solvent is allowed, lending is forbidden, selling movable property is permitted by istihsan, but real estate is excluded.

10 min
Sale of Orphan's Real Estate and Trading Restrictions
Explains conditions for selling orphan's real estate and the prohibition on trading with profit disposition dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which later Hanafi scholars permit a guardian to sell orphan's real estate, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding profit from unauthorized trading.

Core Idea
Real estate sale for orphan requires one of three conditions; unauthorized trading yields profit that must be given in charity (majority view).

This passage continues the discussion on the guardian's authority, focusing on two crucial topics: the sale of real estate and the prohibition on trading for personal profit. For real estate, earlier scholars allowed sale for a minor, but later scholars restricted it to three conditions. For trading, an intra-Hanafi difference arises: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the profit to be given in charity, while Abu Yusuf allows the guardian to keep it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish earlier and later scholars' positions on real estate sale
Earlier scholars (salaf) said the guardian can sell real estate for a minor just like movables. Later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) restricted it to three conditions. Identify these conditions.
The fatwa is according to the later scholars.
2
List the three conditions for selling real estate
Condition 1: buyer offers less than market value (رغب المشتري فيه بضعف القيمة). Condition 2: minor needs the cash (للصغير حاجة إلى أثمنها). Condition 3: deceased's debts cannot be paid otherwise (دين لا وفاء له إلا به).
The text places 'or' between them; any one suffices.
3
Analyze the ruling on trading with orphan's wealth
The guardian is prohibited from trading for himself. If he does, he guarantees the capital, and the profit is subject to dispute: majority say give in charity, Abu Yusuf says keep.
The mufta bihi is the majority view (charity).
4
Understand the rationale for the real estate restrictions
Real estate is self-fortified (muḥṣan bi-nafsihi) and does not decay quickly, so preservation does not require sale unless a specific need exists.
The three conditions represent such needs.

Commentary

Key Terms
العقار (al-'iqār): Immovable property (real estate), which is 'fortified by itself' and thus generally not to be sold by the guardian unless conditions met.
ضعف القيمة (ḍi'f al-qīma): Less than the market value; one of the three conditions for selling real estate.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the mufta bihi rule regarding a guardian who trades with orphan's money for his own profit?
A) He keeps the profit and does not give charity.

C) He returns the capital and profit to the orphan.
D) He is not liable for any loss.

Key Takeaway
A guardian may sell orphan's real estate only under one of three conditions (need, buyer offering less than value, debt); unauthorized trading requires profit charity.

Guardian vs. Grandfather: Priority in Managing Orphan's Wealth
States the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i dispute on priority between father's guardian and grandfather.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the father's appointed guardian takes precedence over the grandfather, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Hanafi: father's guardian beats grandfather; Shafi'i: grandfather takes priority.

This passage concludes with an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that the father's appointed guardian (waṣī) has more right to manage the minor's wealth than the grandfather. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i gives priority to the grandfather, arguing that the Sharī'a places the grandfather in the father's position. This difference has significant practical implications for who controls the orphan's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputing positions
Hanafi: 'Waṣī ab al-ṭifl aḥaqq min al-jadd' – father's guardian is more entitled than grandfather. Shafi'i: 'al-jadd aḥaqq' – grandfather is more entitled.
The text mentions only the Shafi'i view as opposing.
2
Understand each school's proof
Hanafi proof is implicit: the father's choice of guardian is to be honored. Shafi'i proof: the grandfather is placed in the father's position by the Sharī'a (e.g., in inheritance).
The text does not elaborate Hanafi proof; it simply states the difference.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi law, the grandfather cannot override the father's appointed guardian. In Shafi'i law, the grandfather takes precedence and can override.
This affects who has the final say in managing the orphan's property.
4
Note the tarjih
The author states the Hanafi position as mufta bihi without further justification.

[١]قوله : ويحتال ؛ أي يقبلُ الحوالة في قبض الدين الذي كان لليتيم على الآخر. [٢] قوله : على الأملأ ؛ أي على الأقدر على أداء الدين من المحيل وهو المديون. [٤]قوله : إلا العقار ؛ أي يجوز للوصي أن يبيع على الكبير الغائب في كلّ شيء إلا العقار، قيد الكبير بالغيبة ؛ لأنهم إذا كانوا حضوراً ليس للوصي التصرف في التركةِ أصلاً، لكن يتقاضى ديون الميت، ويقبضُ حقوقه، ويدفع إلى الورثة، إلا إذا كان على الميت دين أو أوصى بوصيّة ولم يقض الورثة الديون، ولم ينفذوا الوصية من مالهم، فإنه يبيع التركة. كذا في «العناية». ثم استثنى المصنف العقار في حق الكبير الغائب، أما في حق الصغير فيملك الوصي بيع العقار أيضاً، وهذا جواب السلف. وأما جواب المتأخرين أنه إنما يجوز بأحدِ شروط ثلاثة : إما أن يرغب المشتري فيه بضعف القيمة، أو للصغير حاجة إلى أثمنها، أو ما يكون على الميت دين لا وفاء له إلا به ، قال الصدر الشهيد الله : وبه يفتي. كذا في «الكفاية». (1) قوله : لأن بيع ماله.... الخ ؛ وكان القياس أن لا يملك الوصي بيع غيره غير العقار أيضاً ؛ لأنه لا يملكه الأبُ على الكبير، إلا أنا استحسناه لما أنه حفظ ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه، وحفظ الثمن أيسر، وهو يملك الحفظ. أما العقار فمحصن بنفسه، فلا حاجة فيه إلى البيع ، ولو كان عليه دين يبيعُ العقار، ثم إن كان الدين مستغرقاً باعَ كله بالإجماع، وإن لم يكن مستغرقاً باع بقدر الدين عندهما ؛ لعدم الحاجة إلى أكثر من ذلك. وعند الأعظم الله جاز له بيعُ كلّه ؛ لأنه يبيعه بحكم الولاية، فإذا ثبت في البعض ثبت في الكلّ ؛ لأنها لا تتجزأ، ولو خيف هلاكه يملك بيعه ؛ لأنه تعين حفظه كالمنقول والأصح أنه لا يملك ؛ لأنه نادر. كذا أفاد الزيلعي ) [٢]قوله : ولا يتجر في ماله ؛ لأنّ المفوض إليه... الخ ؛ قال قاضي خان : ولا يجوز للوصي أن يتجر لنفسه بمال اليتيم والميت، فإن فعل وربح ضَمِنَ رأس المال ، ويتصدق بالربح في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له سلّم الربح، ولا يتصدق بشيء. انتهى. [٣] قوله : ووصي أب الطفل أحقُّ بماله من الجد ؛ هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي له الجد أحق ؛ لأنَّ الشرع أقامه مقامَ الأب عند عدمه، حتى أحرز الميراث، فيقدم على وصي الأب.
He may accept a transfer of debt (hawala) from the solvent one, not from the insolvent; he may not lend, and he may sell to a major absent person except real estate. It is permissible for the father, but not for his agent nor the guardian, to sell the property of one minor to another; if the father appoints two agents for that, it is permissible; regarding the judge's sale of that, there is a difference. If the father appoints a man to sell his property to his child or to buy from him, it is not permissible unless the father is present; it is not permissible for the judge to sell the orphan's property to himself; the opposite [is the case] because permissibility from the judge is by way of judgment, and his judgment for himself is not permissible, unlike what he buys from his guardian or sells to the orphan, or accepts the guardian's [sale], for it is permissible; even if [the judge is] a guardian appointed by this judge... etc., summarized. So in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: 'wa yah'tāl' – meaning he accepts the hawala in collecting the debt that was owed to the orphan from the other. [2] His saying: 'ala al-amla'' – meaning the one most capable of paying the debt from the transferor (muhīl), who is the debtor. [3] His saying: 'wa la yuqrid' – meaning the guardian does not lend the orphan's wealth because he is entrusted with preservation, and lending is not preservation; rather it involves harm to the orphan, so it is not permissible. [4] His saying: 'illa al-'iqār' – meaning it is permissible for the guardian to sell to a major absent person in everything except real estate; [the author] restricted [the ruling] to the major by absence because if they are present, the guardian has no authority to dispose of the estate at all; but he collects the debts of the deceased, receives his rights, and pays to the heirs, unless the deceased had a debt or bequeathed a will and the heirs did not pay the debts or execute the will from their own wealth; then he sells the estate. So in al-'Ināyah. Then the author excepted real estate in the case of a major absent person; as for the case of a minor, the guardian owns the sale of real estate as well – this is the answer of the predecessors. As for the answer of the later scholars, it is that it is permissible only under one of three conditions: either the buyer desires it at less than its value, or the minor has a need for its price, or the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid except through it. Al-Sadr al-Shahīd said: and by this fatwa is given. So in al-Kifāyah. (1) His saying: 'because selling his property...' etc. The qiyās would be that the guardian does not own the sale of other than real estate as well, because the father does not own it over the major; but we applied istihsān because it is preservation, since corruption quickly reaches it, and preserving the price is easier, and he owns preservation. As for real estate, it is fortified by itself, so there is no need to sell it. If there is a debt, he sells real estate. Then if the debt is comprehensive, he sells all of it by consensus; if not comprehensive, he sells only to the extent of the debt according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muhammad) – because there is no need for more than that. According to the Greatest (Imām), he is permitted to sell all of it because he sells by virtue of guardianship, so if it is established for part, it is established for the whole, because it is indivisible. If its destruction is feared, he owns its sale because its preservation is determined like movable property; but the most correct is that he does not own it because it is rare. So related al-Zayla‘ī. [2] His saying: 'and he does not trade with his wealth' – because he is entrusted with preservation... etc. Qāḍī Khān said: It is not permissible for the guardian to trade for himself with the wealth of the orphan or the deceased; if he does so and makes a profit, he guarantees the capital, and he gives the profit in charity according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muhammad; according to Abū Yūsuf, he takes the profit and does not give anything in charity. End. [3] His saying: 'The guardian of the minor's father has more right to his wealth than the grandfather' – this is according to us; according to al-Shāfi‘ī, the grandfather has more right, because the Sharī‘a placed him in the position of the father when he is absent, even inheriting [through him], so he is preferred over the father's guardian.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the limits of a guardian's (wasī) authority over an orphan's property. The core principle is that the guardian is entrusted with preservation (ḥifẓ), not profit or speculation. Several rulings are derived from this principle. First, the guardian may accept a transfer of debt (ḥawāla) only from a solvent debtor (al-amla'), not an insolvent one, because this facilitates collection without risk. Second, he may not lend the orphan's money, as lending is not preservation and involves potential harm. Third, he may sell the orphan's movable property to a major absent person (al-kabīr al-ghā'ib) but not real estate (al-'iqār) according to the matn; the commentary explains that real estate is 'fortified by itself' (muḥṣan bi-nafsihi) and thus needs no sale for preservation. However, for a minor, earlier scholars (salaf) allowed sale of real estate, while later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) restricted it to three conditions: (1) the buyer offers less than market value, (2) the minor has a genuine need for the cash, or (3) the deceased's debts cannot be paid otherwise – and this is the fatwa position. The commentary also discusses the father's unique authority to buy/sell between his minor children, which his agent or guardian cannot do without his presence. Regarding trading (tijāra) with the orphan's wealth, it is prohibited for the guardian to trade for his own account; if he does and profits, he guarantees the principal and gives the profit in charity according to Abū Ḥanīfa and Muhammad al-Shaybānī, while Abū Yūsuf allows him to keep the profit. Finally, a dispute with al-Shāfi‘ī is noted: in the Hanafi school, the father's appointed guardian takes precedence over the grandfather in managing the minor's wealth, whereas al-Shāfi‘ī gives priority to the grandfather because the Sharī‘a treats him as the father's substitute in inheritance.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-waṣī): The guardian appointed by the father to manage the minor's property.
الجد (al-jadd): The grandfather (paternal), who in Shafi'i law takes priority over the appointed guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on the priority between the father's appointed guardian and the grandfather?
A) The grandfather has priority.
B) The father's appointed guardian has priority.
C) They share authority equally.
D) The judge decides.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the father's appointed guardian takes priority over the grandfather, contrary to the Shafi'i school which favors the grandfather.

Guardian's Prohibition from Trading and the Istihsan Exception
Ruling with proof and istihsan exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a guardian cannot trade with orphan's property and explain how istihsan permits selling perishable movables.

Core Idea
A guardian is entrusted with preservation, not trade; but istihsan allows selling perishables to prevent loss.

The passage addresses the guardian's authority over an orphan's property. The default ruling is a strict prohibition on trading or selling movable goods for the guardian's own benefit. However, the Hanafi school introduces an exception based on istihsan (juristic preference) to allow the sale of items that are prone to spoilage, prioritizing preservation over the general prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the primary ruling
The text begins with 'ولا يتجر في ماله' – the guardian should not engage in trade with the orphan's property. This is a clear prohibition.
The guardian's role is custodial, not commercial.
2
Identify the rationale
The reason given is 'لأن المفوض إليه الحفظ لا التجارة' – the guardian is entrusted with preservation, not trade. His duty is to protect and preserve, not to profit.
This is the legal cause (illa) for the prohibition.
3
Analyze the qiyas baseline
The author notes that by strict analogy (qiyas), the guardian would not be allowed to sell even movable goods (manqulat), because even the father (the natural guardian) does not have the right to sell an adult child's property. This is the baseline ruling.
Qiyas would extend the prohibition to all sales.
4
Understand the istihsan exception
The Hanafi school departs from qiyas using istihsan. They permit the sale of movable goods that are prone to spoilage (like food or perishable items) because preservation demands quick conversion to cash. The rationale: 'لتسارع الفساد إليه' – due to rapid spoilage. Selling prevents total loss.
Istihsan overrides qiyas for a more suitable outcome.
5
Note the limits of the exception
The exception is limited to preserving the property. The guardian may sell only to prevent loss, not for his own profit. The price is easier to preserve than the actual item.
The guardian still cannot trade for personal gain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتجر في ماله)) ؛ لأن المفوض إليه الحفظ لا التجارة. (1) قوله : لأن بيع ماله.... الخ ؛ وكان القياس أن لا يملك الوصي بيع غيره غير العقار أيضاً ؛ لأنه لا يملكه الأبُ على الكبير، إلا أنا استحسناه لما أنه حفظ ؛ ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه، وحفظ الثمن أيسر، وهو يملك الحفظ. [٢]قوله : ولا يتجر في ماله ؛ لأنّ المفوض إليه... الخ ؛ قال قاضي خان : ولا يجوز للوصي أن يتجر لنفسه بمال اليتيم والميت، فإن فعل وربح ضَمِنَ رأس المال ، ويتصدق بالربح في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له سلّم الربح، ولا يتصدق بشيء. انتهى.
And he should not trade with his property because what is entrusted to him is preservation, not trade. (1) His statement: 'Because selling his property... etc.'; The analogy would be that the guardian does not have the right to sell anything other than real estate as well, because the father does not have that right over the adult, except we approved it based on istihsan because it is preservation; due to the rapid spoilage of it, and preserving the price is easier, and he has the right of preservation. [2] His statement: 'And he should not trade with his property'; because what is entrusted to him... etc.; Qadi Khan said: It is not permissible for the guardian to trade on his own account with the property of the orphan and the deceased; if he does so and makes a profit, he is liable for the principal and must give the profit in charity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, the profit is permissible for him and he does not give anything in charity.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the guardian's (wasi) authority over an orphan's property. The text first states the ruling that the guardian should not trade (ولا يتجر في ماله) because his mandate is preservation (hifz), not trade (tijara). The commentary then explains that by analogy (qiyas), the guardian should not even be allowed to sell movable property (manqulat) other than real estate, since the father himself does not have that right over an adult child. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to permit the sale of perishable movables to prevent their loss, as preservation is easier with cash. The second part quotes Qadi Khan on the intra-Hanafi dispute: if the guardian trades with the orphan's property for his own benefit, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require him to guarantee the capital and give the profit in charity, while Abu Yusuf allows him to keep the profit altogether. This shows that the guardian's role is strictly custodial.

Key Terms
ولا يتجر (wa lā yatajjar): The guardian must not engage in trade with the orphan's property for his own benefit.
استحسناه (istaḥsannāhu): We (Hanafis) approved it based on istihsan, departing from qiyas for a more suitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the guardian has full authority over the property.
B) Because the father permitted it.
C) Because preserving perishable goods overrides the analogy (istihsan).
D) Because the orphan can be consulted.

Key Takeaway
A guardian is prohibited from trading with an orphan's property, but istihsan permits selling perishable goods to prevent loss, reflecting the priority of preservation.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Profit from Unauthorized Trade
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit disposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding the disposition of profit from unauthorized trade with orphan's property.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require charity; Abu Yusuf permits profit—showing the scope of guardian's liability.

The passage continues with a specific scenario: a guardian trades with the orphan's property for his own benefit and makes a profit. The Hanafi school is split on what happens to that profit. This intra-madhhab difference highlights the tension between strict custodial duty and the possibility of entrepreneurial benefit, and it affects how we assess the guardian's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
The passage states: if a guardian trades with the orphan's property for his own account and makes a profit, what must he do?
This assumes the trade is unauthorized and for personal gain.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the guardian must guarantee the principal (i.e., replace any loss) and give the profit in charity (sadaqah). He cannot keep it.
They view the guardian as a trustee (amin) who cannot benefit from the property.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that the profit is permissible (halal) for the guardian to keep. He is not required to give anything in charity.
Abu Yusuf considers the guardian as an agent (wakil) who can enjoy the fruits of his labor, akin to a mudarib.
4
Analyze the basis of each view
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad emphasize the guardian's role as a pure trustee (hifz), so any profit from the trust property belongs to the trust (orphan) or must be purified via charity. Abu Yusuf sees the guardian as having some entrepreneurial capacity, so profit is his compensation.
The difference reflects two conceptions of guardianship.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the majority view, the guardian must return the profit to charity; under Abu Yusuf, he keeps it. This affects the guardian's behavior and incentives.
The mufta bihi position is likely the majority view, but Abu Yusuf's opinion may be followed in some contexts.

Source Text

Selling Real Estate by the Guardian for Debt: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guardian's sale of real estate for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on selling real estate for debt and distinguish the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The guardian's authority to sell real estate is limited: the Sahibayn restrict to the debt amount, while Abu Hanifa allows selling all.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator discusses when a guardian may sell the orphan's real estate. While movable property is sold by istihsan due to spoilage, real estate requires a special need. The main dispute arises when the debt is partial: should the guardian sell only enough to cover the debt, or the entire property? We will examine the arguments of Abu Hanifa and his two students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Real estate is considered self-preserving (muḥṣin bi-nafsihi), so it is not normally sold. The guardian's authority is restricted to necessary cases.
Movable property is treated differently due to rapid spoilage.
2
Recognize the exception for debt
If the deceased owes a debt, the guardian may sell real estate to pay it. When the debt exhausts the entire estate, there is consensus (ijma') that all can be sold.
This is the context for the dispute.
3
Analyze the dispute over partial debt
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that only the amount needed to cover the debt may be sold. Abu Hanifa holds that the entire property may be sold because the authority to sell is indivisible.
The reasoning hinges on whether need restricts the sale or the guardianship extends it.
4
Consider the case of fear of destruction
If the property is at risk of being destroyed, the guardian may sell it, but the soundest view (al-ashahh) is that he may not, as such cases are rare.
This shows the preference for preserving the orphan's assets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) قوله : لأن بيع ماله.... الخ ؛ وكان القياس أن لا يملك الوصي بيع غيره غير العقار أيضاً ؛ لأنه لا يملكه الأبُ على الكبير، إلا أنا استحسناه لما أنه حفظ ؛ ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه، وحفظ الثمن أيسر، وهو يملك الحفظ. أما العقار فمحصن بنفسه، فلا حاجة فيه إلى البيع ، ولو كان عليه دين يبيعُ العقار، ثم إن كان الدين مستغرقاً باعَ كله بالإجماع، وإن لم يكن مستغرقاً باع بقدر الدين عندهما ؛ لعدم الحاجة إلى أكثر من ذلك. وعند الأعظم الله جاز له بيعُ كلّه ؛ لأنه يبيعه بحكم الولاية، فإذا ثبت في البعض ثبت في الكلّ ؛ لأنها لا تتجزأ، ولو خيف هلاكه يملك بيعه ؛ لأنه تعين حفظه كالمنقول والأصح أنه لا يملك ؛ لأنه نادر. كذا أفاد الزيلعي )
(1) His statement: 'Because selling his property... etc.'; The analogy would be that the guardian does not have the right to sell anything other than real estate as well, because the father does not have that right over the adult, except we approved it based on istihsan because it is preservation; due to the rapid spoilage of it, and preserving the price is easier, and he has the right of preservation. As for real estate, it is self-preserving, so there is no need for its sale. And if there is a debt, he sells the real estate. Then, if the debt is exhaustive, he sells all of it by consensus. If it is not exhaustive, he sells only to the extent of the debt according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because there is no need for more. According to the Greatest (Abu Hanifa), it is permissible for him to sell all of it, because he sells by virtue of guardianship; thus if it is established for part, it is established for the whole, because it is indivisible. And if its destruction is feared, he has the right to sell it because its preservation becomes necessary like movable property, but the soundest view is that he does not have that right because it is rare. Thus al-Zayla'i stated.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of real estate by a guardian (wasi) to pay off a debt. The text first explains that by analogy (qiyas), the guardian should not be able to sell any property at all, but istihsan allows selling movable goods due to rapid spoilage. Real estate, being self-preserving, normally should not be sold. However, if the deceased owes a debt, then the guardian may sell real estate. There is a consensus (ijma') that if the debt covers the entire estate, the guardian may sell all. The dispute arises when the debt is less than the full value: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that only the amount necessary to cover the debt may be sold. Abu Hanifa, referred to as 'الأعظم' (the Greatest), allows selling all of it on the grounds that the guardianship (wilaya) is indivisible; the authority to sell part extends to the whole. The text also mentions a case where the property is at risk of destruction: the guardian may sell, but the preferred view (al-ashahh) is that he may not, as such cases are rare. This highlights the Hanafi principle of preferring careful preservation of the orphan's assets.

Key Terms
العقار (al-'iqār): Real estate, immovable property.
العظم (al-A'ẓam): A respectful title for Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the deceased's debt is less than the value of the real estate, how much can the guardian sell?
A) All of it
B) Only the portion equal to the debt
C) None of it
D) Half of it

Key Takeaway
The guardian's sale of real estate is only for necessity, and the extent is debated—Sahibayn restrict to the debt, Abu Hanifa allows full sale.

Preference of Father's Executor over Grandfather
Ruling that father's executor is more entitled than grandfather, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the father's executor is more entitled than the grandfather, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The father's will transfers his wilaya to the wasi, making him like the father himself.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over who should manage an orphan's property when the father has appointed an executor. The Hanafi position gives priority to the executor, while the Shafi'i school favors the grandfather. Understanding the reasoning behind each view deepens your appreciation of how different usul al-fiqh principles lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the explicit ruling in the text: the father's executor is more entitled (أحق) to the child's property than the grandfather.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the grandfather to be more entitled, based on the legal substitution of the grandfather for the father.
Al-Shafi'i argues the law places the grandfather in the father's position.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the grandfather inherits and has marriage guardianship, indicating he stands in the father's place.
Opposing dalil: 'الشرع أقامه مقام الأب عند عدمه'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the father's appointment (īṣā') transfers his wilaya to the executor, making the executor's authority a continuation of the father's.
The father's choice, knowing the grandfather exists, indicates the executor is more beneficial.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude the practical difference: where a wasi exists, he manages property; the grandfather only takes over if no wasi is appointed.
The grandfather still retains non-financial roles like marriage guardianship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووصي أب الطفل أحقُّ بما له من الجد، فإن لم يكن له وصي فالجد ( ووصي أب الطفل أحقُّ بما له من الجد" ، فإن لم يكن له وصى فالجد. [٣] قوله : ووصي أب الطفل أحقُّ بماله من الجد ؛ هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي له الجد أحق ؛ لأنَّ الشرع أقامه مقامَ الأب عند عدمه، حتى أحرز الميراث، فيقدم على وصي الأب.  فإن لم يكن له وصي فالجد حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية فإن لم يكن له وصى فالجد". ولنا : إنّ بالإيصاء تنتقل ولاية الأب إلى الوصي، فكانت ولايته قائمة معنى، فيقدم الوصي على الجد : كالأب نفسه، وهذا لأنَّ اختيار الأب الوصي مع علمه بقيام الجد يدل على أن تصرف الوصي أنظر لبنيه من تصرُّف أبيه. هذا ما يفهم من كلام صاحب «الهداية»(۱). ] قوله : فإن لم يكن له وصي فالجد ؛ أي بمنزلة الأب ؛ لأنه أقرب الناس إليه، وأشفقهم عليه ، حتى ملك الإنكاح دون الوصي، غير أنه يقدم عليه وصي الأب في التصرف لما بيناه آنفاً. كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
And the father's executor of the child is more entitled to his property than the grandfather. If he has no executor, then the grandfather. [3] His statement: 'And the father's executor of the child is more entitled to his property than the grandfather'; this is our (Hanafi) view. According to al-Shafi'i, the grandfather is more entitled because the law places him in the position of the father in his absence, such that he inherits, so he takes precedence over the father's executor. [PAGE_646] 'If he has no executor, then the grandfather' - Husn al-Diraya ... 'If he has no executor, then the grandfather.' Our proof: By the appointment (īṣā'), the father's guardianship (wilāya) transfers to the executor, so his guardianship effectively continues. Thus the executor takes precedence over the grandfather like the father himself. This is because the father's choice of an executor, despite knowing that the grandfather exists, indicates that the executor's conduct is more beneficial for his children than the grandfather's conduct. This is understood from the words of the author of al-Hidaya. [His statement]: 'If he has no executor, then the grandfather'; meaning he is in the position of the father because he is the closest person to him and most compassionate, such that he has the right to give in marriage (inkāḥ) without the executor. However, the father's executor takes precedence over him in financial dealings, as we explained earlier. Thus in al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 645

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding who is more entitled to manage an orphan's property: the father's appointed executor (wasi al-ab) or the grandfather (jadd). The Hanafi position, as stated, is that the father's executor takes precedence (أحق) over the grandfather. If there is no executor, then the grandfather takes charge. Al-Shafi'i opposes this, arguing that the grandfather is more entitled because Islamic law places him in the father's stead when the father is absent; for example, the grandfather inherits and has the right to give the orphan in marriage without the executor. The Hanafi response (ولنا) is that through the father's appointment (al-īṣā'), the father's guardianship (wilāya) is transferred to the executor, so the executor's authority is effectively a continuation of the father's. Thus the executor is like the father himself in precedence. Moreover, the father's deliberate choice of an executor, knowing that the grandfather is available, shows that the father considers the executor more beneficial (anẓar) for his children than his own father. The text then notes that the grandfather, if there is no executor, is considered like the father in closeness and compassion, but the father's executor still surpasses him in financial matters. This ruling is taken from al-Hidaya.

Key Terms
وصي الأب (waṣī al-ab): The executor appointed by the father to manage the children's affairs after his death.
أحق (aḥaqq): More entitled, having stronger right.
ولاية (wilāya): Guardianship, legal authority over a ward's person or property.
إيصاء (īṣā'): The act of appointing an executor or making a will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The grandfather
B) The father's executor
C) They are equal
D) The mother

Key Takeaway
The father's executor is more entitled than the grandfather because the father's guardianship transfers to the executor, making him the father's representative.

Rulings for a Hermaphrodite Before Clarification
States prohibitions and obligations for a hermaphrodite until sex is determined

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the precautionary rulings (ihtiyat) for a hermaphrodite whose sex is unknown, and explain the reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
The hermaphrodite is treated with caution until sex is clear—avoiding potential haram takes priority over permissible actions.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a set of rulings for a khuntha (hermaphrodite) whose sex has not become apparent. These rulings are not absolute prohibitions but precautionary measures (ihtiyat) to avoid falling into what may be haram. By studying this lesson, you will understand how the Hanafi school applies caution when certainty is lacking, and you will be able to articulate the specific restrictions and their justifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default rulings
First, understand the general rulings for men and women regarding silk, jewelry, seclusion, travel, circumcision, and funeral washing. This establishes the baseline.
For example, silk is haram for men, halal for women.
2
Recognize the uncertainty of the hermaphrodite's sex
The khuntha's sex is unknown, so we cannot definitively apply either set of rulings. This triggers the need for precaution.
3
Apply the principle of ihtiyat
The principle is: prefer the stricter option to avoid potential sin. So if an act is haram for one sex, it is prohibited for the khuntha; if it is wajib for one sex, it may be required.
The text says 'abstaining from the forbidden is obligatory, engaging in the permissible is permissible'.
4
Analyze each specific ruling
Go through each prohibition and obligation: silk, jewelry, seclusion, travel, circumcision, and death washing. Identify the reasoning given in the commentary.
E.g., silk is disliked because if he is male, it is haram; precaution avoids that.
5
Note the special cases of circumcision and death
Circumcision requires purchasing a slave girl (or state provision) and then selling her. At death, tayammum is performed instead of washing with water.
These are exceptional measures because of the unique situation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلبس حريراً وحلياً، ولا يكشف عند رجل وامرأة، ولا يخلو به غير محرم رجل أو امرأة، ولا يسافر بلا محرم، وكره للرَّجُلِ والمرأة ختنه، وتُبْتاعُ أمة تختِنُهُ إن ملك مالاً، وإلا فمن بيت المال، ثم تُباعُ، فإن مات قبل ظهور حاله لم يغسل ويتيمم. من التّيمم، وهو جعلُ الغيرِ ذا تيمم، وإنما لا يشتري له جارية تغسله؛ لأن الجارية لا تكون مملوكة له بعد الموت، إذ لو كانت لجاز غسل الجارية لسيدها إذا لم يكن خنثى، وكان هذا أولى من غسل الرّجل الرجل.
[1] قوله : ولا يلبس حريراً؛ وإنّما كره ذلك؛ لأن لبسه حرام على الرجال، وحالته مجهولة بعد، فيؤخذ بالاحتياط، فإنّ الاجتناب عن الحرام فرض، والإقدام على المباح مباح، فيكره اللبس حذر الوقوع في الحرام، وكُفْنُ كالمرأة للاحتياط أيضاً. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «شرح المجمع».
[2] قوله : ولا يكشف.... الخ؛ لا يقال : انكشاف العورة حرام مطلقاً، فما معنى لتخصيصه بالخنثي؛ لأنا نقول : المراد بالانكشاف هاهنا أن يكون في إزار واحد، لا إبداء موضع العورة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا «الأكملية».
[3] قوله : وتبتاع؛ أي تشترى له أمة تختنه إن له مال؛ لأنه يباح لمملوكته النظر إليه رجلاً كان أو امرأة. كذا في «الهداية».
[4] قوله : ثم تباع؛ أي بعد تمام مصلحة الختن تباع الأمة، ويردُّ ثمنها إلى بيت المال كما كان.
He should not wear silk or jewelry, and should not uncover before a man or woman, and a non-mahram man or woman should not be alone with him, and he should not travel without a mahram. It is disliked for both man and woman to circumcise him. A slave girl is purchased to circumcise him if he has wealth, otherwise from the public treasury, then she is sold. If he dies before his condition becomes apparent, he is not washed and tayammum is performed. From at-tayammum, which is making another have tayammum. And a slave girl is not purchased to wash him because the slave girl is not owned by him after death, for if she were, it would be permissible for the slave girl to wash her master if he was not a hermaphrodite, and this would be more appropriate than a man washing a man. [1] His saying: 'He should not wear silk' – it is disliked because wearing it is forbidden for men, and his state is unknown, so precaution is taken. Indeed, abstaining from the forbidden is obligatory, and engaging in the permissible is permissible, so wearing it is disliked out of fear of falling into the forbidden, and he is shrouded like a woman also out of precaution. [2] His saying: 'He should not uncover...' – it cannot be said that uncovering the private parts is absolutely forbidden, so what is the meaning of specifying it for the hermaphrodite? For we say: the intended meaning of uncovering here is being in a single garment, not exposing the place of the private parts. [3] His saying: 'A slave girl is purchased' – meaning a slave girl is bought for him to circumcise him if he has wealth, because it is permissible for his female slave to look at him whether he is a man or a woman. [4] His saying: 'Then she is sold' – meaning after the benefit of circumcision is complete, the slave girl is sold and its price is returned to the public treasury as before.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, specifically a set of precautionary measures for a hermaphrodite (khuntha) whose sex is unknown. The text states that he should not wear silk or jewelry because silk is haram for men, and until his sex is determined, we take the precautionary path (ihtiyat). Similarly, he should not be alone with a non-mahram of either sex, and he should not travel without a mahram. Interestingly, it is disliked (makruh) for either a man or a woman to circumcise him, as circumcision is specific to males, and performing it on an uncertain female would be problematic. Therefore, a slave girl is purchased to perform the circumcision if he has wealth; otherwise, the state provides it. The slave girl is then sold. Regarding death, if he dies before clarification, he is not given a full ritual wash (ghusl) but only dry ablution (tayammum), because washing with water would require determining sex (male washes female? etc.), so caution dictates tayammum. The commentary by al-Jalabi explains that the prohibition of silk is due to the general prohibition for men, and since his sex is unknown, we avoid it. The commentary also clarifies that 'uncovering' here means being in a single garment, not exposing the private parts, because the latter is always haram. The entire set of rulings is built on the principle of ihtiyat (precaution) to avoid potential haram. This is a clear expression of the Hanafi method of applying caution when a ruling is uncertain.

Key Terms
خنثى (khuntha): A person with ambiguous genitalia, whose sex is not clearly male or female.
احتياط (ihtiyat): The principle of taking the precautionary measure when the ruling is uncertain, preferring the stricter option to avoid potential sin.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with dust or earth performed when water is unavailable or cannot be used, here used for a deceased person whose sex is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a hermaphrodite not given a regular ghusl (ritual wash) after death before their sex is determined?
A) Because water is not available.
B) As a precaution, because washing requires knowing the sex to assign the correct washer, and tayammum avoids potential sin.
C) Because they are considered impure.
D) Because they are not entitled to a Muslim burial.

Key Takeaway
The hermaphrodite before clarification is governed by ihtiyat: avoid what may be haram, and perform what is necessary with caution, until sex is known.

Validity of Gesture for a Mute and Temporarily Speechless Person
Inter-madhhab dispute on gesture validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between a mute and a temporarily speechless person regarding the validity of gesture, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on incapacity alone.

Core Idea
A gesture is valid only when it is customary and understood; a temporary condition lacks established signs.

This passage addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can a person who is temporarily unable to speak use gestures or writing to make legally binding expressions (e.g., acknowledgements, wills, divorces)? The answer hinges on whether mere incapacity suffices or whether the gesture must be a known and habitual means of communication. Let's unpack the Hanafi position and its proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school holds: a congenital mute's gesture/writing is valid; a temporarily speechless person's is not, unless the condition becomes chronic and gestures become known.
The source explicitly contrasts the two cases.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that both are valid because the only reason (illa) for allowing alternative expression is incapacity, which applies equally.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i argument is based on the principle that inability to speak (ʿajz) is the cause for allowing gestures, and no distinction is made between permanent and temporary inability.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the ʿilla.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response introduces an additional condition: the gesture must be 'customary and understood' (ma'huda ma'luma). This is only true for a congenital mute; a temporarily speechless person has no established signs. However, if the condition persists and signs become known, he is treated like a mute.
This shows the Hanafi reliance on customary practice in determining legal capacity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a temporarily speechless person cannot effectuate a transaction (e.g., sale, divorce) via gesture, while under Shafi'i he can. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.
Understand why this difference matters in daily fiqh practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل شتى
كتابة الأخرس وإيماؤه
قوله: مسائل شتى؛ اعلم أنّ ذكر مسائل شتى أو مسائل متفرقة، أو مسائل منثورة ونحوها من دأب المصنفين؛ لتدارك ما لم يذكر فيما كان بحق ذكره. كذا في «العناية».
قوله: كتابة الأخرس وإيماؤه؛ مبتدأ، وكالبيان خبره، صورة المسألة: إنه إذا قُرءَ على الأخرس كتاب وصيّة فقيل له: أتشهد عليك بما في هذا الكتاب فأوما برأسه: أي نعم، أو كتب، فإذا جاء من ذلك ما يعرف أنّه إقرار فهو جائز؛ لكونه كالبيان، ولا يجوز ذلك في الذي يعتقل لسانه. وقال الشافعي رضي الله عنه يجوز في الوجهين؛ لأنّ المجوز إنّما هو العجز، وقد شمل الفصلين ولا فرق بين الأصلي والعارضي، والفرق لأصحابنا أن الإشارة إنما تعتبر إذا صارت معهودة معلومة، وذلك في الأخرس دون المعتقل لسانه، حتى لو امتد ذلك الاعتقال، وصارت له إشارات معلومة، قالوا: هو بمنزلة الأخرس. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله: وإيماؤه؛ أي إشارتُه، أما كونها حجّة في حق الأخرس في حق هذه الأحكام؛ للحاجة إلى ذلك؛ لأنها من حقوق العباد، ولا يختص بلفظ دون لفظ، فإنّه كما يثبت بالعربي يثبت بغيره، وقد ثبت بدون اللفظ كما في بيع التعاطي ونكاح الفضولي مع القدرة على التكلّم، فلأن ثبت هاهنا والعجز متحقق أولى. ولقائل أن يقول: من هذه الأحكام الطلاق على ما صرح به بقوله: ونكاحه وطلاقه، وهو من حقوق الله تعالى؛ لأنّ فيه تحريم الفرج، وهو حق الله تعالى؛ ولهذا لم يشترط الدعوى في الشهادة عليه بالاتفاق.
ولا بد في كون الإشارة حجّة من أن يكون الحكم في حقوق العباد فقط، أو مما
Miscellaneous issues. The writing of the mute and his gesture. He said: 'Miscellaneous issues': know that mentioning miscellaneous issues or scattered issues or dispersed issues is the habit of authors to make up for what was not mentioned where it should have been. As in al-'Inayah. He said: 'The writing of the mute and his gesture': it is a subject, and 'like the explanation' is its predicate. The form of the issue: If a will document is read to a mute, and he is asked: 'Do you testify to what is in this document?' and he gestures with his head (i.e., yes), or writes, and from that it is known that it is an acknowledgement, it is valid because it is like explanation. But it is not valid for one whose tongue is temporarily tied. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) said it is valid in both cases because the enabler is incapacity, which includes both cases, and there is no difference between permanent and temporary. The distinction for our school is that gesture is considered only when it becomes customary and understood, which applies to the mute but not the temporarily speechless. However, if the temporary condition persists and his gestures become understood, they said he is like a mute. As in al-Hidayah. He said: 'And his gesture' i.e., his sign. As for it being a proof in the case of the mute regarding these rulings, it is due to necessity because these are rights of the people, and they are not confined to a specific utterance. Just as it is established in Arabic, it is established in other languages, and it has been established without speech as in mu'atah sale and marriage by a guardian without his consent even when able to speak. So it is more appropriate to be established here where incapacity is present. Someone might object: among these rulings is divorce, as he explicitly stated: 'and his marriage and divorce', and that is from the rights of Allah because it involves prohibition of the private part, which is Allah's right; therefore, a lawsuit is not required for testimony on it by agreement. And it is necessary for the gesture to be a proof that the ruling is only in the rights of the people, or from ...

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal capacity of a mute person to express his will through gesture or writing. The Hanafi position is that such expression is valid if it is habitual and understood. The passage contrasts this with the Shafi'i view, which makes no distinction between permanent and temporary speech incapacity. The Hanafi distinction is based on the need for customary understanding: a mute's gestures are known, while a temporarily speechless person's gestures are not yet established. However, if the temporary condition becomes longstanding and gestures become known, the rule applies. The passage also addresses the objection that some of these rulings (like divorce) are rights of Allah, not just of people, but the argument is that gesture is still valid due to necessity.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is born mute or permanently unable to speak
الإيماء (al-īmā'): Gestural communication, e.g., nodding or pointing, used as a substitute for verbal expression
المعتقل لسانه (al-mu'taqal lisānihi): A person who has a temporary speech impediment (e.g., due to illness or injury)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the gesture of a temporarily speechless person considered legally valid?
A) Immediately upon losing speech
B) Only if he is born mute
C) If the condition persists and his gestures become understood
D) Never, even if gestures become understood

Key Takeaway
A gesture is legally valid only when it is a customary and understood means of communication; this condition distinguishes the permanent mute from the temporarily speechless in Hanafi fiqh.

The Speech-Impeded Person: Definition and Hanafi Ruling on Gestures
Definition and inter-madhhab dispute on speech-impeded person's gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mu'takil al-lisān, state the Hanafi conditions for treating his gestures like those of a mute, and identify the relied-upon position on the duration.

Core Idea
A speech-impeded person's gestures are valid only if the impediment is prolonged and his signs are understood.

Can a person who temporarily cannot speak use gestures to conclude a contract or grant a divorce? This lesson defines the mu'takil al-lisān (speech-impeded person) and explains the Hanafi school's conditional treatment of his gestures. We will also contrast the Hanafi view with Shafi'i and identify the operative fatwa on the required duration of the impediment.

Guided Path
1
Define the term
Identify the subject: mu'takil al-lisān is someone whose tongue is confined, preventing speech temporarily or permanently.
Distinguish from the naturally mute (akhras).
2
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shāfi‘ī equates the speech-impeded person with a mute unconditionally, making all gestures valid.
No conditions required.
3
State the Hanafi position and conditions
Hanafis require two conditions for the equivalence: the impediment must extend (prolonged) and his signs (ishārāt) must be known/understood.
If either condition fails, gestures are not valid.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on duration
There are two views on 'extension': one year (some Hanafis) or until death (other Hanafis).
The text says 'qīl' (it is said) for the second view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that the impediment must last until death.
The text states 'wa ‘alayhi al-fatwā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتقلُ اللسان : هو الذي عرض له احتباس اللسان حتى لا يقدر على الكلام، فعند الشَّافِعِي له حكمه حكم الأخرس ، وعند أصحابنا إن إمتدَّ ذلك وعُلِمَ إشاراته كان حكمه حكم الأخرس وإلا فلا، وقدر الإمتداد بسنة ، وقيل : بأن يبقى إلى زمان الموت ، قيل : وعليه الفتوى.
The speech-impeded person: he is the one who experiences confinement of the tongue so that he is unable to speak. According to al-Shāfi‘ī, his ruling is the ruling of the mute. According to our companions (the Hanafis), if that extends and his signs are known, his ruling is the ruling of the mute; otherwise not. The extent of the extension is one year, and it is said: until the time of death. It is said: and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the mu'takal al-lisān (person with a speech impediment). It opens by defining the term: one who suffers from a temporary or permanent confinement of the tongue preventing speech. The text then presents an inter-madhhab dispute. Imam al-Shāfi‘ī equates this person entirely with a mute (akhras) in all legal matters. The Hanafi school (our companions – i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) imposes two conditions for this equivalence: (1) the impediment must extend for a significant period, and (2) his gestures (ishārāt) must be commonly understood. If both conditions are met, his gestures and writing are valid like those of a mute. Otherwise, they are not. The duration of extension is specified as one year according to the dominant view, with another view that it must last until death. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the latter view (until death). This ruling is crucial for transactions, testimony, and divorce where the person's intent must be known.

Key Terms
المعتقل اللسان (al-mu‘takil al-lisān): A person whose tongue is confined so that he cannot speak temporarily or permanently.
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is naturally or permanently mute.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): Gestures used to communicate intent, such as nodding for affirmation or denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view on the required duration of a speech impediment for gestures to be valid?
A) One year
B) Six months
C) Until death
D) Until recovery

Key Takeaway
A speech-impeded person's gestures are valid under Hanafi law only if the impediment extends until death (mufta bihi) and his signs are known.
