Hanafi Ruling on Mixed Flock: Taharrī and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on when slaughtered and dead animals are mixed, with conditions for eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of taḥarrī to a mixed flock of slaughtered and dead animals, and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
When dead animals are fewer, Hanafis permit eating after investigation, unlike Shafi'is who require necessity.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario: a flock contains both properly slaughtered animals and dead ones. The Hanafi school permits eating after careful investigation (taharrī) when the dead are fewer, invoking the principle of removing hardship. You will also encounter the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response based on analogy to Muslim markets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State the Hanafi position: when dead animals are fewer, one investigates (taharrī) and eats in the state of choice (ikhtiyār). If dead are more or equal, one does not eat. In necessity (iḍṭirār), even dead animals become permissible.
The ruling hinges on the proportion of dead to slaughtered.
2
Understand the condition of the dead being fewer
The text specifies 'aqall' (fewer). If dead are fewer, investigation is required. If dead are more or equal, eating is not allowed in choice.
This condition determines whether taharrī is needed.
3
State the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that taharrī is a proof of necessity (dalīl ḍarūrī). Since one can abstain from meat without hardship, there is no necessity, so eating is not permissible.
The dispute centers on the scope of taharrī.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that taharrī is used to remove hardship (ḥaraj), not only in necessity. They analogize to Muslim markets where prohibited items exist yet people eat relying on the predominant lawful (ghalabah).
This response relies on istiḥsān and qiyas.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: eating after taharrī is permissible in normal circumstances. Shafi'i: only permissible in necessity. This affects daily life where mixed flocks are common.
The difference is not merely theoretical; it changes permissibility in routine situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي غنم مذبوحة فيها ميتة ، وهي أقل تحرّى وأكل في الاختيار، إنما قال : في الاختيار ؛ لأنَّه يَحِلُّ أَكلُ وهي الميتة في حال الاضطرار، وقال الشَّافِعِي له : لا يباحُ التناول ؛ لأنَّ التَّحري دليل ضروري، ولا ضرورة هنا. قلنا: التحري يصار إليه لدفع الحرج ، وأسواق المسلمين لا تخلـو عــن المسروق، والمغصوب والمحرم ، ومع ذلك يباحُ التناول اعتمادا على الغالب. أقل؛ أي الميتة أقل تحرّى وأكل، أما إن كانت الميتة أكثر أو كانا نصفين لم يؤكل، وهذا إذا كانت الحالة حالة الاختيار ؛ أي بأن يجد ذكية بيقين، أما في حالة الضرورة يحلّ له التناول في جميع ذلك ؛ لأنّ الميتة المتيقنة تحل له في حالة الضرورة. فالتي تحتمل أن تكون ذكيّة أولى غير أنه يتحرى ؛ لأن التحري يوصله إلى الذكية في الجملة، فلا يتركه بلا ضرورة. كذا في «الهداية».
And regarding a flock of sheep, some slaughtered and some dead: if the dead are fewer, one investigates and eats in the state of choice. He only said 'in the state of choice' because eating the dead animal is permitted in the state of necessity. Al-Shāfi‘ī said: consumption is not permissible because investigation (taḥarrī) is a proof of necessity, and there is no necessity here. We said: investigation is resorted to in order to remove hardship, and the markets of Muslims are not free from stolen, usurped, and prohibited items, yet consumption is permitted relying on the majority. 'Fewer' means the dead are fewer, so one investigates and eats. But if the dead are more or equal, it is not eaten. This is when the situation is one of choice, i.e., when one finds a definitely slaughtered (dhakiyyah) animal. In the state of necessity, consumption is permitted in all cases because the definitely dead animal is permitted in necessity. So what is likely to be slaughtered is more deserving, except that one should investigate because investigation leads to the slaughtered animal in general, so one should not leave it without necessity. Thus in al-Hidāyah.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a common problem: a mixed flock of slaughtered and dead animals. The matn states: if the dead animals are fewer in number, one may investigate (taḥarrī) and eat in normal circumstances (ikhtiyār). The commentator specifies that this is only in choice because in necessity (iḍṭirār) even a known dead animal becomes permissible. The text then records a dispute with Imam al-Shāfi‘ī. Al-Shāfi‘ī argues that investigation is a proof of necessity and cannot be used without a genuine necessity. The Hanafi response is that investigation is employed to remove hardship (ḥaraj), and by analogy, Muslim markets contain many prohibited items yet people are allowed to buy and eat based on the predominant lawful majority. This is the principle of ghalabah (predominance). The ruling is then detailed: if the dead animals are more or equal, one may not eat in choice. If one is in necessity (no other food), any animal becomes permissible. Even in necessity, one should investigate to reach the slaughtered animal if possible. This passage is from al-Hidāyah and shows the Hanafi use of istiḥsān and consideration of public ease.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taḥarrī): The act of investigating to determine the most likely permissible item when things are mixed.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): The normal state where one has access to permissible food without extreme need.
الاضطرار (al-iḍṭirār): A state of dire necessity that permits normally forbidden acts, such as eating carrion.
ذكية (dhakiyyah): An animal that has been properly slaughtered according to Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required before eating from a mixed flock where the dead are fewer?
A) Eat without investigation
B) Investigate and choose the most likely slaughtered
C) Avoid all meat
D) Only eat in necessity

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a mixed flock with fewer dead animals permits eating after investigation, based on the principle of removing hardship and analogy to markets.
